As someone who has very little cycling experience, I‚Äôve learned a couple of things while cycling through PA this week: 1) Cycling in the rain is not fun, but cycling through the rain in heavy wind is even less fun. 2) Lubing the bike is super important, and if the brake is not properly lubed it might stop working while you are speeding down a hill into traffic. 3) Trying to ride a tandem bike up a mountain with no gears is pretty much impossible, but you will get an excellent upper body workout while you push it to the top. After hiking out of Hot Springs I got attacked by hornets, stung all over and covered in welts. I took a benadryl and hiked another 25 miles feeling like a zombie and pushing past the fatigue. The next day I climbed into the Smoky Mountains. I started to ascend the 6,000ft mountain and the flood gates opened and it poured on me all 17 miles on my way to the shelter. I was shaking in my hawaiian shorts, T-shirt, and rain jacket. The wind pushed me around and I could barely feel my hands, everything was soaked and freezing. The shelter was just recently opened up again because of a few bear attacks which caused it to close over the summer. I sat in the shelter and tried to keep warm, curled up in my emergency mylar blanket and any dry clothes I had left. The next morning I hiked out and snow and ice covered the trees. Even in all of the cold and rain, I enjoyed the BEAUTY that was created. I crossed the 2000 MILE mark and a huge smile came upon my face! I can‚Äôt believe I‚Äôve walked 2000 miles! I get this overwhelming feeling of JOY out here and I wish the day would never end. I remember thinking of all the small things that have given me joy. The nice afternoon naps under the sun, the guillotine rock, the little minnows in the puddle pushing around my water bottle, or even when I accidentally caught a fish! I pinched myself, how incredible it was to sit out on the edge of this cliff and listens to one of my favorite movies being performed live. Post_ID: 2132395987838163077	|Post_Code: B2Xytpfj9iF	|Owner_ID: 3558793026	|Likes: 16	|Time_Stamp: 1568421432	|Tags: ['#appalachiantrail', '#atsobo2019', '#rvtravel', '#halfway']	|Caption: Why is my son eating all that ice cream? We're proud to let you know. #appalachiantrail #atsobo2019 #rvtravel #halfway  @c4web88 @misskaymay<br>Photos courtesy of Coach TenBears "Half Gallon Challenge<br>by&nbsp;Cumberland Valley Visitors Bureau<br><br>A play on words has never tasted so sweet! The Cumberland Valley is home to the midway point of the 2,180-mile&nbsp;Appalachian Trail. This famous trail includes some famous traditions. Historically when thru-hikers pass near the midway point they will stop at the Pine Grove Furnace General Store (located in&nbsp;Pine Grove Furnace State Park) and chow down on a half-gallon of Hershey's ice cream.<br><br>It might seem like an easy feat for a thru-hiker, some known to burn around 5,000 calories a day while hiking the famed Georgia to Maine footpath. However, the brick of ice cream can still be considered a "challenge" for most.<br><br>What do hikers walk (or hike) away with? A commemorative wooden spoon stamped with "Member of the Half Gallon Club" and the sweet smell of success. <br>Visitors to the Pine Grove Furnace General Store can enjoy flipping through the composition notebook filled with hiker tales of attempted and finished half-gallons. Some brag they could "still eat a cheeseburger" while others dream of "sleeping it off on the trail." While visiting Pine Grove Furnace State Park plan a stop at the&nbsp;Appalachian Trail Museum, the only museum in the country dedicated to hiking. This museum highlights the unique stories of the trail and the people that have traversed it. You will learn about interesting people like Grandma Gatewood who was the first solo female hiker to complete the entire trail at age 67. <br>This museum also features examples of A.T. shelters and thru-hiker memoirs. If you stop by on the right day you can mingle with the thru-hikers in the lounge area.<br><br>Author:&nbsp;Cumberland Valley Visitors Bureau"

That‚Äôs right, I hiked the ‚Äú4 STATE CHALLENGE‚Äù!!! 44 miles within 24hours. We woke up at 1:30am, strapped our headlamps on and broke down camp as quick as we could. I feel free, independent, strong, happy, connected, simple, and just right when I‚Äôm in the woods. It‚Äôs scary and tiring and boring and really hard sometimes. The people you meet on the trail are immediately your new best friends who help you and are there for you and you will do anything to help them too. You eat for fuel. You smell and you are caked in layers of dirt. Your body aches from head to toe but a night‚Äôs rest will usually do the trick (usually). The best part is that when you are out there, you gain perspective. You have time to think.  Your interactions and thoughts are deep.  You are not connected to your phone, not stressed out at work. You breathe fresh mountain air with every breath, and you drink water from cold streams.  You don‚Äôt want to go home. I am beyond excited to spend 6 months this year hiking the Appalachian Trail with the person who I trust, love, and look up to most. He will be hiking it on his 10 year anniversary of thru-hiking it north-bound when he had just finished college. We will start in Maine in late June and walk home (and then some!), perhaps in time for Thanksgiving with family.
